## 8. Conclusions and Further Research

### 8.1 Summary of Achievements

1. **Positive characterizations:** We defined odd and prime numbers through their additive properties, rather than through the absence of certain properties.

2. **$\mathrm{AUI}(n)$ index:** We introduced and investigated the Almost Uniformity Index, which provides a complete and positive condition for primality.

3. **Constructive method:** We developed a theoretical method for generating prime numbers through construction of their additive profiles, providing an alternative to sieve methods.

4. **Experimental confirmation:** We conducted calculations confirming the correctness of our theoretical considerations.

5. **Additive entropy:** We proposed an information-theoretic measure for quantifying the complexity of additive structures.

### 8.2 Directions for Further Research

| Direction | Description |
|:----------|:------------|
| **Algorithm optimization** | Further work on reducing the computational complexity of the constructive method |
| **Generalizations** | Investigation of similar characterizations for other classes of numbers (semiprimes, composite numbers with special structure) |
| **Connections to number-theoretic hypotheses** | Study of relationships between additive profiles and open problems such as the Goldbach conjecture or the Riemann hypothesis |
| **Additive entropy** | Further development and investigation of entropy-based measures of additive complexity |
| **Algebraic structures** | Investigation of algebraic structures related to additive profiles (additive semigroups, tensor structures) |

### 8.3 Perspectives

The presented approach opens new perspectives in number theory, connecting additive and multiplicative properties in a coherent theory. Although the proposed method for generating prime numbers is not currently computationally competitive with existing methods, its **theoretical value is invaluable**—it shows that prime numbers can be characterized and generated in a purely positive way, based on their internal additive structure.

# Constructive Additive Theory of Natural Numbers

**Author:** Kajetan Młynarski  
**Source:** Preprint on ResearchGate  
**Related Codex Modules:** XVI, XIX, XXII, XXXIV

---

## Abstract

Classical number theory develops in a multiplicative language: divisor, product, the Sieve of Eratosthenes, greatest common divisor. In this language, additive problems like the Goldbach conjecture become unnatural and difficult to describe, leading to barriers known as the parity problem. This paper proposes an alternative: a number theory built in a purely additive language, where the structure of natural numbers emerges from positive composition relations rather than negative divisibility conditions. We introduce the Additive Regularity Index (ARI), prove that ARI(n) = 1 if and only if n is prime, and show that the set {2, 3} constitutes a canonical seed from which all natural numbers can be additively generated. We develop a hierarchical construction algorithm, introduce concepts of additive entropy and synergy, and analyze "gappy universes" (numerical semigroups) generated by alternative seeds. The Goldbach conjecture emerges as a special case of a more general constructive consistency principle.

**Keywords:** additive number theory, constructive generation, additive entropy, synergy, numerical semigroups, Goldbach conjecture

---

## 1. Introduction: Two Languages of Number Theory

Classical number theory develops in a **multiplicative language**:
- Divisor, product, the Sieve of Eratosthenes, greatest common divisor, prime factorization

In this language, additive problems like the Goldbach conjecture become unnatural and difficult to describe, leading to barriers known as the **parity problem**. The multiplicative language is excellent for describing what numbers *are made of* (factors), but awkward for describing how they *combine* (sums).

In this work, we propose an alternative: a number theory built in a **purely additive language**, where the structure of natural numbers emerges from **positive composition relations** rather than negative divisibility conditions.

> **Key Insight:** Instead of asking "What divides n?" (negative: n has no divisors), we ask "How can n be built from smaller atoms?" (positive: n = a₁ + a₂ + ... + aₖ).

---

## 2. Additive Atom and Regularity Index

### 2.1 Additive Profile

> **Definition 1 (Additive Profile).** For a natural number $n \in \mathbb{N}$, we define the *additive profile* $\mathcal{P}(n)$ as the set of all non-increasing sequences of natural numbers $(a_1, \ldots, a_k)$, $k \geq 1$, such that:
> $$a_1 + \cdots + a_k = n$$

### 2.2 Almost-Uniform Partition

> **Definition 2 (Almost-Uniform Partition).** An *almost-uniform partition* is an additive decomposition of $n$ containing exactly two distinct components differing by 1. Formally: a sequence $(a_1, a_2, \ldots, a_k)$ is almost-uniform if:
> $$\{a_1, \ldots, a_k\} = \{m, m+1\}$$
> for some $m \in \mathbb{N}$, and both values occur at least once.

### 2.3 Additive Regularity Index (ARI)

> **Definition 3 (Additive Regularity Index).** Let $n > 2$. Denote by $J(n)$ the number of partitions of $n$ that are almost-uniform, counted for all lengths $k \in \{2, \ldots, n-1\}$. We define:
> $$\mathrm{ARI}(n) = \frac{J(n)}{n - 2}$$
> with $\mathrm{ARI}(2) = 1$ by definition.

> **Theorem 1 (Additive-Multiplicative Bridge).** The following equivalence holds:
> $$\mathrm{ARI}(n) = 1 \iff n \text{ is a prime number}$$

*Proof sketch.* For prime $p$, for each $k \in \{2, \ldots, p-1\}$, the unique representation $p = a \cdot k + r$ with $0 < r < k$ yields an almost-uniform partition consisting of $(k - r)$ components equal to $a$ and $r$ components equal to $a+1$. For composite $n$, if $k$ divides $n$, the uniform partition $(n/k, \ldots, n/k)$ is not almost-uniform, creating a deficit in $J(n)$. Full proof appears in [1, 2]. $\square$

### 2.4 Set of Additive Atoms

> **Definition 4 (Set of Additive Atoms).** Let:
> $$\mathcal{A}_1 = \{n \in \mathbb{N} : \mathrm{ARI}(n) = 1\}$$
> The set $\mathcal{A}_1$ corresponds to the classical set of prime numbers.

---

## 3. Canonical Construction of $\mathbb{N}$

The two smallest elements of $\mathcal{A}_1$ are **2 and 3**. They constitute the minimal seed from which the entire set of natural numbers greater than 1 can be additively generated.

> **Definition 5 (Canonical Seed).** The set $\{2, 3\}$ is called the *canonical seed* of the additive system.

> **Lemma 1 (Generativity Principle).** Since $\gcd(2, 3) = 1$, every $n \geq 2$ can be written as a non-negative combination $2a + 3b = n$. In particular, the set $\{2, 3\}$ additively generates $\mathbb{N}_{\geq 2}$.

> **Definition 6 (Additive Rank).** For a fixed set of atoms $\mathcal{A}_1$, we define:
> $$r_{\mathcal{A}_1}(n) = \min\{k : n = a_1 + \cdots + a_k,\ a_i \in \mathcal{A}_1\}$$

**Observation 1 (Minimal Constructive Rules).** For the set $\{2, 3\}$:
- Even numbers $n \geq 4$ have minimal rank $r = 2$
- Odd numbers $n \geq 7$ have minimal rank $r = 3$

---

## 4. Hierarchical Construction Algorithm

> **Algorithm 1 (Additive Hierarchical Construction Algorithm)**

1. **Initialization:** Let $\mathcal{A}_1 = \{2, 3\}$, $\mathcal{N} = \emptyset$

2. **For $n = 4, 5, 6, \ldots$ do:**

   (a) **Compute $\mathrm{ARI}(n)$:**
       - Generate all partitions of $n$
       - Count almost-uniform partitions $J(n)$ for $k = 2$ to $n-1$
       - Compute $\mathrm{ARI}(n) = J(n) / (n-2)$ for $n > 2$

   (b) **If $\mathrm{ARI}(n) = 1$ then:**
       - Add $n$ to $\mathcal{A}_1$ (discovery of new atom)

   (c) **Compute additive rank:**
       $$r(n) = \min\{k : \exists a_1, \ldots, a_k \in \mathcal{A}_1,\ a_1 + \cdots + a_k = n\}$$

   (d) **Classify:** Determine minimal representation of $n$ in the system

3. **End** when desired construction range is reached

> **Hypothesis 1 (Constructive Consistency Hypothesis).**
> - For even $n \geq 4$, $r_{\mathcal{A}_1}(n) = 2$
> - For odd $n \geq 7$, $r_{\mathcal{A}_1}(n) \leq 3$, with $r_{\mathcal{A}_1}(n) = 2$ if and only if $n-2$ is prime

The Goldbach conjectures (weak and strong) thus represent special cases of this general constructive consistency principle.

---

## 5. Synergy and Additive Entropy

Why should the system obey minimal rank rules? One possible answer comes from the concepts of **entropy** and **additive synergy**.

> **Definition 7 (Additive Entropy).** For a number $n$, we define $H_{\text{add}}(n)$ as the Shannon entropy of the frequency distribution of components across all possible partitions of $n$. Let $C(n)$ be the multiset of all components from all partitions in $\mathcal{P}(n)$, and $N(n) = |C(n)|$. For $a \in \{1, \ldots, n\}$, let:
> $$p(a) = \frac{\#\{a \in \mathcal{P}(n) \mid a \text{ appears}\}}{N(n)}$$
> Then:
> $$H_{\text{add}}(n) = -\sum_{a=1}^{n} p(a) \log_2 p(a)$$
> The normalized additive entropy is defined as $\tilde{H}_{\text{add}}(n) = H_{\text{add}}(n) / \log_2 n$.

> **Definition 8 (Additive Synergy).** For a pair of numbers $(a, b)$ such that $a + b = N$, we define synergy as:
> $$S(a, b) = H_{\text{add}}(a) + H_{\text{add}}(b)$$
> An alternative correlational measure is:
> $$S'(a, b) = H_{\text{add}}(a + b) - |H_{\text{add}}(a) - H_{\text{add}}(b)|$$

**Observation 2 (Properties of Entropy and Synergy).** Preliminary calculations for small $n$ confirm the following properties:

- Prime numbers have **lower entropy** $H_{\text{add}}(p)$ than composite numbers of similar size
- Goldbach pairs $(p_1, p_2)$ exhibit **higher synergy** $S(p_1, p_2)$ than mixed pairs $(p, c)$ where $c$ is composite
- The "non-Goldbach" state is characterized by **lower total system synergy**

Full verification of these properties over large ranges of $n$ constitutes the first step of the research program.

---

## 6. Gappy Universes and Numerical Semigroups

We can consider seeds $S = \{a, b\}$ other than $\{2, 3\}$. The system then additively generates the set:
$$\langle S \rangle = \{xa + yb : x, y \in \mathbb{N}\}$$
called a **numerical semigroup**.

> **Theorem 2 (Frobenius Number).** If $\gcd(a, b) = 1$, then the largest number unreachable in $\langle S \rangle$ is:
> $$g = ab - a - b$$
> All numbers $n > g$ belong to $\langle S \rangle$.

**Table 1: Examples of Alternative Seeds and Generated Semigroups**

| Seed $S$ | $\gcd(a,b)$ | Frobenius $g$ | System Character | Smallest elements of $\langle S \rangle$ |
|:---------|:------------|:--------------|:-----------------|:----------------------------------------|
| $\{2,3\}$ | 1 | 1 | full from $n \geq 2$ | 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, ... |
| $\{5,7\}$ | 1 | 23 | "gappy" to 23 | 5, 7, 10, 12, 14, ... |
| $\{6,9\}$ | 3 | — | only multiples of 3 | 6, 9, 12, 15, ... |
| $\{4,7\}$ | 1 | 17 | partial | 4, 7, 8, 11, 12, ... |

**Observation 3 (Properties of Alternative Systems).** Studying "gappy universes" generated by alternative seeds enables:

- Analysis of $\mathrm{ARI}(n)$ behavior within constrained semigroups
- Study of additive rank distributions $r(n)$ in partial systems
- Construction of entropy and synergy hierarchies in different additive structures
- Classification of systems according to their emergent properties

These gappy universes with large $g$ model arithmetic structures with limited energy/information availability, creating a natural connection with the synergy concept.

---

## 7. Summary and Research Program

The presented approach replaces the classical **Parity Problem** with a new **Additive Consistency Principle**. Natural numbers emerge here not as a ready-made set, but as the result of an iterative generative process based on simple addition relations.

### 7.1 Research Program

| Step | Description |
|:-----|:------------|
| **Step 1 (Empirical)** | Verification of synergy properties over large ranges of $n$ — confirming that $S(p_1, p_2) > S(p, c)$ for Goldbach pairs |
| **Step 2 (Theoretical)** | Proof of a formal Synergy Lemma demonstrating the superiority of pure prime pairs in terms of system entropy |
| **Step 3 (Systemic)** | Establishment of a fundamental lower bound for system entropy $H_{\text{system}}(N)$ and demonstration of contradiction with the "non-Goldbach" state |
| **Step 4 (Generalizations)** | Classification and study of "gappy universes" generated by alternative seeds |

> **The Goldbach conjecture thus ceases to be an isolated problem — it becomes a consequence of the internal balance of a system that from the very beginning determines its own constructive rules.**

---

# Additive Physics of Natural Numbers: Atoms, Orbitals, and the Unified Goldbach Principle

**Author:** Kajetan Młynarski  
**Source:** Preprint on ResearchGate  
**Related Codex Modules:** XVI, XIX, XXII, XXXIV, XLIV

---

## Abstract

Classical number theory is predominantly multiplicative in character. Fundamental concepts like prime numbers are defined negatively (having no proper divisors), and additive problems such as Goldbach's conjecture appear as isolated anomalies in a multiplicative landscape. This paper proposes an alternative: the **Additive Physics of Natural Numbers (AFN)**. This theory:

- Takes the additive semigroup $\langle 2,3 \rangle$ as fundamental
- Defines primes positively as minimal stable additive configurations
- Derives number-theoretic properties from global structural constraints
- Unifies weak and strong Goldbach conjectures under one principle

We introduce the canonical additive profile, hierarchical atomism (first-, second-, third-, and fourth-order atoms), and a five-component architecture that reduces the Goldbach search to 14 structured candidate pairs. The theory provides a physical interpretation of additive processes, treating composite numbers as "excited states" that decay to stable prime configurations, and Goldbach pairs as "entangled states" in the additive field.

**Keywords:** additive physics, Goldbach conjecture, prime numbers, semigroup, canonical profile, atomic hierarchy

---

## 1. Introduction: The Multiplicative Paradigm and Its Limitations

Classical number theory is predominantly **multiplicative** in character. Fundamental concepts like prime numbers are defined negatively (having no proper divisors), and additive problems such as Goldbach's conjecture appear as isolated anomalies in a multiplicative landscape. This approach faces well-known barriers, notably the **parity problem** in sieve methods.

We propose an alternative: the **Additive Physics of Natural Numbers (AFN)**. This theory:

- Takes the additive semigroup $\langle 2,3 \rangle$ as fundamental
- Defines primes positively as minimal stable additive configurations
- Derives number-theoretic properties from global structural constraints
- Unifies weak and strong Goldbach conjectures under one principle

> **Key Shift:** Instead of asking "What divides n?" (multiplicative, negative), we ask "How can n be built from 2s and 3s?" (additive, positive).

---

## 2. The Additive Semigroup $\langle 2,3 \rangle$

> **Definition 2.1 (Additive Semigroup).** The additive semigroup $\langle 2,3 \rangle$ is defined as:
> $$\langle 2,3 \rangle = \{2a + 3b \mid a,b \in \mathbb{N}\}$$

> **Proposition 2.2 (Completeness).** For every $n \geq 2$, $n \in \langle 2,3 \rangle$. The semigroup is minimal and cofinite.

> **Definition 2.3 (Additive Profile).** For $n \in \langle 2,3 \rangle$, an *additive profile* is a pair $(a,b) \in \mathbb{N}^2$ such that:
> $$n = 2a + 3b$$

> **Lemma 2.4 (Parity Lemma).** For $n = 2a + 3b \in \langle 2,3 \rangle$:
> $$n \equiv b \pmod{2}$$
> Thus: $b$ even $\Rightarrow n$ even; $b$ odd $\Rightarrow n$ odd.

### 2.1 Canonical Additive Profile

Since the representation $n = 2a + 3b$ is not unique, we introduce a **canonical choice**.

> **Definition 2.5 (Canonical Additive Profile).** Let $n \geq 2$. A pair $(a,b) \in \mathbb{N}^2$ is called the *canonical additive profile* of $n$ if:
> $$n = 2a + 3b,\qquad a \equiv n \pmod{3}$$
> and $a$ is the smallest nonnegative integer with this property. We write $(a,b) = \mathrm{Prof}_{\mathrm{can}}(n)$.

> **Proposition 2.6 (Existence and Uniqueness).** For every $n \geq 2$, the canonical additive profile $\mathrm{Prof}_{\mathrm{can}}(n)$ exists and is unique.

*Proof sketch.* The condition $n - 2a \equiv 0 \pmod{3}$ is equivalent to $a \equiv n \pmod{3}$. Among all nonnegative integers in this residue class modulo 3, there is a unique minimal one. For that $a$, $b = (n - 2a)/3$ is uniquely determined and nonnegative. $\square$

In particular, for every even $2N$ there is a unique canonical profile:
$$2N = 2a + 3b,\qquad (a,b) = \mathrm{Prof}_{\mathrm{can}}(2N)$$
which will serve as the basis of the five-component architecture.

---

## 3. Hierarchical Atomism: Orders of Additive Structure

### 3.1 First-Order Atoms: The Additive Basis

> **Definition 3.1 (First-Order Atoms).** The set $\mathcal{A}_1 = \{2, 3\}$ constitutes the *first-order additive atoms*. These generate $\langle 2,3 \rangle$ and cannot be further decomposed within the semigroup.

### 3.2 Second-Order Atoms: Prime Numbers

> **Definition 3.2 (Second-Order Atoms).** A number $p > 1$ is a *second-order atom* (prime number) if:
> 1. Its additive profile $p = 2a + 3b$ is minimally complex (locally minimal $a + b$)
> 2. It admits no stable additive decomposition $p = x + y$ with $1 < x, y < p$
> 3. It is multiplicatively irreducible

**Example 3.3.** Prime numbers and their additive profiles:

| Prime | Additive Profile | $a$ | $b$ |
|:------|:-----------------|:---:|:---:|
| 5 | $2 + 3$ | 1 | 1 |
| 7 | $2 + 2 + 3$ | 2 | 1 |
| 11 | $2 + 3 + 3 + 3$ | 1 | 3 |
| 13 | $2 + 2 + 3 + 3 + 3$ | 2 | 3 |

### 3.3 Higher-Order Atomic Structures

> **Definition 3.4 (Third-Order Atoms).** Numbers representable as sums of three primes:
> $$\mathcal{A}_3 = \{p + q + r \mid p, q, r \in \mathcal{A}_2\}$$
> These correspond to odd numbers $\geq 7$ (weak Goldbach territory).

> **Definition 3.5 (Fourth-Order Atoms).** Numbers representable as sums of two primes:
> $$\mathcal{A}_4 = \{p + q \mid p, q \in \mathcal{A}_2\}$$
> These correspond to even numbers $\geq 4$ (strong Goldbach territory).

---

## 4. Axiomatization of Additive Physics (AFN)

> **Axiom 4.1 (First-Order Atomism).** There exist exactly two first-order additive atoms: $\mathcal{A}_1 = \{2, 3\}$. The semigroup $\langle 2,3 \rangle$ generated by $\mathcal{A}_1$ constitutes the entire space of natural numbers $\geq 2$.

> **Axiom 4.2 (Additive Profiling).** Every natural number $n \geq 2$ possesses an additive profile $n = 2a + 3b$ with $(a,b) \in \mathbb{N}^2$. In particular, it has a canonical profile $\mathrm{Prof}_{\mathrm{can}}(n)$.

> **Axiom 4.3 (Second-Order Atomism).** Prime numbers are second-order atoms: minimal stable configurations of first-order atoms that block both additive and multiplicative decomposition.

> **Axiom 4.4 (Additive Decay).** Every composite number undergoes *additive decay*: $n = x + y$ with $1 < x, y < n$, eventually reaching a stable configuration of second-order atoms.

> **Axiom 4.5 (Orbital Principle).** The minimal number of second-order atoms needed for stable representation depends on the parity of $b$ in the additive profile $n = 2a + 3b$:
> $$\text{Orbital}(n) = \begin{cases} 2 & \text{if } b \text{ is even (n even)} \\ 3 & \text{if } b \text{ is odd (n odd)} \end{cases}$$

> **Axiom 4.6 (Identity Conservation).** A number's identity is defined by its minimal stable atomic configuration. Unstable representations represent transitional processes, not mathematical objects.

> **Axiom 4.7 (Global Uniformity).** The additive space $\langle 2,3 \rangle$ is complete, homogeneous, and structurally coherent. Global properties determine local behavior.

---

## 5. Main Theorems and Structural Results

> **Theorem 5.1 (3-Prime Mechanism — Conditional).** Assuming the 2-prime mechanism for even numbers within the AFN framework, every odd number $N \geq 7$ has a representation as a sum of three primes; that is, it occupies a 3-prime orbital.

*Proof.* For odd $N = 2a + 3b$, $b$ is odd. Then:
$$N = 3 + (2a + 3(b-1))$$
where $2a + 3(b-1)$ is even. By the 2-prime mechanism (Theorem 5.2), this even number decomposes into two primes, giving a 3-prime representation of $N$. $\square$

> **Theorem 5.2 (2-Prime Mechanism — Structural).** Within the AFN framework, every even number $2N \geq 4$ is assigned a 2-prime orbital; that is, it is structurally associated with a representation as a sum of two primes, as a consequence of the orbital principle and global uniformity of $\langle 2,3 \rangle$.

> **Theorem 5.3 (Identity Conservation).** Within AFN, a natural number exists as a mathematical object only if it achieves its minimal stable atomic configuration. Numbers without such configuration are interpreted as transitional processes.

> **Theorem 5.4 (Non-Goldbach Impossibility).** In the AFN framework, an even number without a 2-prime representation cannot exist as a stable mathematical object. It would represent a structural defect in the additive field $\langle 2,3 \rangle$.

---

## 6. Physical Interpretation and Connections

### 6.1 Goldbach Pairs as Entangled States

> **Definition 6.1 (Additive Entanglement).** A Goldbach pair $(p,q)$ with $p + q = 2N$ is *additively entangled* if:
> 1. The identity of $2N$ depends on both components
> 2. Changing one component immediately determines the other
> 3. The pair represents a minimal-energy configuration

> **Proposition 6.2.** All Goldbach pairs are additively entangled states of the field $\langle 2,3 \rangle$.

### 6.2 Additive Decay as Physical Process

Composite numbers behave like **excited states** in quantum systems:
- They decay to more stable configurations (sums of primes)
- The decay chain is finite and deterministic
- Energy is measured by additive entropy $H(n) = a + b$

### 6.3 Global Constraints and Local Behavior

The AFN framework aligns with constraint-based physics:
- Local number properties emerge from global field constraints
- The semigroup $\langle 2,3 \rangle$ acts as a constraint field
- Goldbach representations are **necessary**, not contingent, within AFN

---

## 7. The Five-Component Architecture

> **Definition 7.1 (Canonical Five-Component Profile).** For even $2N \geq 4$, let $(a,b) = \mathrm{Prof}_{\mathrm{can}}(2N)$ be its canonical additive profile, so that:
> $$2N = 2a + 3b$$
> The associated *canonical five-component profile* is the multiset:
> $$(a, a, b, b, b)$$
> and the identity:
> $$2N = a + a + b + b + b$$
> is a direct decomposition of the canonical profile into five elementary additive components.

> **Proposition 7.2 (Fourteen Repartitions).** The canonical five-component profile admits exactly **14 ordered binary repartitions** into candidate Goldbach pairs $(X,Y)$ obtained by partitioning the multiset $\{a, a, b, b, b\}$ into two nonempty submultisets. This number arises as:
> $$2^{5-1} - 1 = 14$$
> It coincides numerically with the fourth Catalan number $C_4 = 14$, although the underlying combinatorial structure is different.

> **Theorem 7.3 (Structured Goldbach Search — AFN Reformulation).** For every even $2N$, the AFN framework associates to $2N$ a structured family of **14 candidate pairs** $(X,Y)$ derived from its canonical five-component profile. The strong Goldbach conjecture can be reformulated as the assertion that, for every $2N$, at least one of these 14 pairs is a Goldbach pair $(p,q)$ with $p,q$ prime.

---

4. Developed an axiomatization of AFN
5. Connected number theory with physics of constrained systems

| **Physical Connections** | Deeper exploration of quantum number theory and constraint-based dynamics |

### 8.3 Long-Term Vision

The Additive Physics of Natural Numbers represents more than a solution to specific problems—it offers a new foundation for number theory where:

- Numbers are **active participants** in structural dynamics
- **Global architecture** determines local behavior
- **Physical intuition** guides mathematical discovery
- Additive and multiplicative views find **synthetic unity**

# A Dynamic and Additive Framework for Number Theory: AFN+PFS Core v0.6

**Author:** Kajetan Młynarski  
**Source:** Preprint on ResearchGate  
**Related Codex Modules:** II, XVI, XIX, XXII, XXXIV

---

## Abstract

We present a dynamic and additive framework for number theory, combining Additive Fields of Natural Numbers (AFN) with Potentially Finite Set Dynamics (PFS). AFN provides a static, structural description of additive semigroups in terms of additive atoms, rank, entropy, and the Additive Regularity Index (ARI). PFS offers a dynamic, probabilistic formalism for growth processes with stochastic closure. Their synthesis yields a process-structural theory capable of modeling emergent arithmetic regularities, stability mechanisms, and symmetry formation in additive systems.

In this version, we refine the framework with detailed numerical examples for additive entropy, complete geometric intuition for the instability functional, formalized stability theorems, and an extended example illustrating Goldbach-type dynamics. The framework provides a new language for describing additive phenomena as dynamic stabilization processes.

**Keywords:** additive number theory, PFS, entropy, stability, Goldbach conjecture, dynamical systems

---

## 1. Introduction

Classical number theory predominantly treats natural numbers as static objects endowed with arithmetic relations such as divisibility and primality. The Additive Field of Natural Numbers (AFN) programme reframes this viewpoint: numbers are seen as configurations in an additive field, characterized by additive atoms, additive rank, additive entropy, and regularity indices. Prime numbers emerge as minimal, highly regular additive states, while composite numbers represent excited configurations with higher entropy.

In parallel, the Potentially Finite Set (PFS) framework models dynamic processes where growth, local transformations, and probabilistic closure interact to produce finite-time stabilization. Rather than assuming finiteness a priori, PFS derives it as an emergent property of the process dynamics.

This document presents a unified core framework, **AFN+PFS**, where AFN supplies the static additive geometry and PFS supplies the dynamic stabilization mechanism. The framework reinterprets classical additive problems as questions about the stability and convergence of dynamic processes.

**Key contributions of this version (v0.6):**

- A detailed numerical example of additive entropy computation for the number 10
- Geometric intuition for the instability functional $F$ as a Lyapunov function
- Formalized stability theorems with complete proofs
- An extended example for $2N = 10$ with full numerical analysis and interpretation of stability

---

## 2. Related Work

The AFN+PFS framework intersects several established areas:

| Area | Connection |
|:-----|:-----------|
| **Additive semigroup theory** | Atoms, unique and non-unique factorizations, numerical semigroups |
| **Probabilistic and Markov processes** | Growth models, random stopping times, martingale methods |
| **Information theory and thermodynamics** | Entropy as a measure of degeneracy, free-energy functionals |
| **Structural number theory** | Distribution of primes, additive decompositions, sieve methods |

Unlike standard probabilistic approaches to number theory, AFN+PFS does not merely approximate arithmetic phenomena by random models. Instead, it interprets additive regularities as the outcome of structured dynamic processes on an underlying additive field, where stability is governed by information-theoretic functionals.

---

## 3. Additive Fields of Natural Numbers (AFN)

We formulate AFN in a general setting applicable to commutative semigroups, with the classical case $(\mathbb{N}, +)$ and prime numbers as the primary example.

### 3.1 Additive Semigroups and Atoms

> **Definition 3.1 (Additive Atom).** Let $(S, +)$ be a commutative semigroup with identity $0$ (for simplicity). An element $a \in S$ is an *additive atom* if it cannot be expressed as $a = x + y$ with $x, y \in S \setminus \{0\}$. The set of atoms is denoted $A(S)$.

> **Remark 3.2.** In $(\mathbb{N}, +)$ under the AFN interpretation, additive atoms can be taken either as the set of prime numbers $\mathcal{P}$, or as a minimal generating set such as $\{2, 3\}$ depending on the desired granularity. In both cases, atoms are those elements not further decomposable within the chosen scale.

> **Definition 3.3 (Atomic Completeness).** A semigroup $S$ is *atomically complete* if every $x \in S$ admits at least one finite decomposition into atoms:
> $$x = a_1 + \cdots + a_k, \qquad a_i \in A(S),\; k \geq 0$$

### 3.2 Additive Rank

> **Definition 3.4 (Additive Rank).** For $x \in S$, the *additive rank* of $x$ is defined by:
> $$\operatorname{rank}(x) = \min\{k \geq 0 : x = a_1 + \cdots + a_k,\ a_i \in A(S)\}$$
> By convention, $\operatorname{rank}(0) = 0$.

> **Remark 3.5.** In $(\mathbb{N}, +)$ with atoms chosen as primes, $\operatorname{rank}(p) = 1$ for a prime $p$, while composite $n$ satisfy $\operatorname{rank}(n) \geq 2$. If atoms are $\{2, 3\}$, then $\operatorname{rank}(n)$ is the minimal number of 2's and 3's needed to represent $n$.

### 3.3 Admissible Decompositions and Additive Entropy

> **Definition 3.6 (Admissible Decompositions).** For $x \in S$, let $D(x)$ denote the set of all finite tuples $d = (a_1, \ldots, a_k)$ such that $a_i \in A(S)$ and $a_1 + \cdots + a_k = x$. We consider tuples up to reordering; for uniqueness we assume they are non-decreasing. We call $D(x)$ the set of *admissible decompositions* of $x$.

> **Definition 3.7 (Additive Probability Measure).** Assume $D(x)$ is finite and nonempty. We define a probability measure $p_x$ on $D(x)$. The canonical choice is the uniform distribution:
> $$p_x(d) = \frac{1}{|D(x)|}, \qquad d \in D(x)$$
> This reflects the principle of indifference among decompositions.

> **Definition 3.8 (Additive Entropy).** The *additive entropy* of $x \in S$ with respect to $p_x$ is:
> $$H_{\mathrm{add}}(x) = -\sum_{d \in D(x)} p_x(d) \log p_x(d)$$
> In the uniform case this reduces to $H_{\mathrm{add}}(x) = \log |D(x)|$.

**Example 3.9 (Additive Entropy Computation for 10).** Let $S = \mathbb{N}$ with atoms being prime numbers. We compute $H_{\mathrm{add}}(10)$ step by step.

First, list all admissible decompositions of 10 into primes (non-decreasing tuples):

| Decomposition | Components |
|:--------------|:-----------|
| $2+2+2+2+2$ | five 2's |
| $2+2+3+3$ | two 2's and two 3's |
| $2+3+5$ | 2, 3, 5 |
| $3+7$ | 3, 7 |
| $5+5$ | two 5's |

Thus $|D(10)| = 5$. Under the uniform measure, $p_x(d) = 1/5$ for each decomposition. The additive entropy is:
$$H_{\mathrm{add}}(10) = -\sum_{d \in D(10)} \frac{1}{5} \log \frac{1}{5} = \log 5 \approx 1.609$$

This illustrates how additive entropy measures the diversity of additive structure: 10 has multiple distinct prime decompositions, resulting in relatively high entropy. For comparison, a prime number $p$ has $|D(p)| = 1$ and $H_{\mathrm{add}}(p) = 0$.

### 3.4 Additive Regularity Index (ARI)

The Additive Regularity Index quantifies how close an element is to being an additive atom, combining rank and entropy into a single measure. The definition is motivated by:

- **Atom maximality:** Atoms should have the highest possible regularity, so $\mathrm{ARI}(a) = 1$ for any atom $a$
- **Rank penalty:** Higher rank indicates more complex structure, so ARI decreases with rank
- **Entropy penalty:** Higher entropy indicates more degeneracy, so ARI decreases with $H_{\mathrm{add}}$
- **Normalization:** The index should lie in $(0, 1]$ for easy interpretation

> **Definition 3.11 (Additive Regularity Index).** Let $x \in S$. Assume $S$ is atomically complete and $D(x) \neq \emptyset$. Define:
> $$\mathrm{ARI}(x) = \frac{1}{\operatorname{rank}(x)} \cdot \frac{1}{1 + H_{\mathrm{add}}(x)}$$
> For $x = 0$ we set $\mathrm{ARI}(0) = 1$ by convention.

> **Proposition 3.12 (Basic Properties of ARI).** Assume $S$ is atomically complete and $D(x)$ is finite for all $x$. Then:
> (a) $0 < \mathrm{ARI}(x) \leq 1$ for all $x \in S$;
> (b) $\mathrm{ARI}(a) = 1$ for any atom $a \in A(S)$;
> (c) if $x$ is non-atomic and admits at least two distinct decompositions, then $\mathrm{ARI}(x) < 1$.

*Proof.* (a) For nonzero $x$, $\operatorname{rank}(x) \geq 1$ and $H_{\mathrm{add}}(x) \geq 0$, so $\mathrm{ARI}(x) \leq 1$ and positive. For $x = 0$, the convention gives $\mathrm{ARI}(0) = 1$.

(b) If $a$ is an atom, then $\operatorname{rank}(a) = 1$ and there is exactly one decomposition $a$ itself, hence $H_{\mathrm{add}}(a) = 0$ and $\mathrm{ARI}(a) = 1$.

(c) If $x$ is non-atomic, then $\operatorname{rank}(x) \geq 2$. If $D(x)$ contains more than one decomposition, then $H_{\mathrm{add}}(x) > 0$. Thus each factor in the definition of $\mathrm{ARI}(x)$ is strictly less than 1, hence $\mathrm{ARI}(x) < 1$. $\square$

> **Remark 3.13.** The factor $1/(1 + H_{\mathrm{add}})$ rather than $1/H_{\mathrm{add}}$ or $e^{-H_{\mathrm{add}}}$ is chosen to avoid singularities when $H_{\mathrm{add}} = 0$ and to provide a gentle decay. This functional form reflects the idea that the first bits of entropy cause the largest drop in regularity.

---

## 4. Potentially Finite Sets (PFS)

We recall the PFS formalism in a form adapted to coupling with AFN.

### 4.1 States and Growth

> **Definition 4.1 (PFS State).** A PFS state is a pair $S_k = (X_k, \Sigma_k)$ where:
> - $X_k$ is a primary object (e.g., a number, vector, or decomposition);
> - $\Sigma_k$ is auxiliary structural data, such as rank, entropy, or ARI.

> **Definition 4.2 (Growth Operator).** A *growth operator* at step $k$ is a function:
> $$m_k : S_k \to S_{k+1}$$
> which implements a local transformation, such as modifying a decomposition or adding an atom.

### 4.2 Stability Probability and Stopping Time

> **Definition 4.3 (Stability Probability).** Each state $S_k$ carries a *stability probability* $p(S_k) \in [0, 1]$, interpreted as the probability that the process terminates at step $k$. This probability typically depends on $\Sigma_k$, and we require that for absorbing states $p(S_k) = 1$.

> **Definition 4.4 (Stopping Time).** A *stopping time* for a PFS process is a random variable:
> $$\tau = \min\{k \geq 0 : \text{process stops at } S_k\}$$

> **Remark 4.5.** In typical PFS scenarios, $p(S_k)$ depends explicitly on $\Sigma_k$ (e.g., via ARI or entropy), so that more regular, lower-entropy states are more likely to be absorbing. This creates a feedback loop where the process tends to settle into stable configurations.

### 4.3 A Simple PFS Example

**Example 4.6 (Toy PFS on $\mathbb{N}$).** Let $X_0 \in \mathbb{N}$, and define $X_{k+1} = X_k + 1$ with probability $1 - q$, while with probability $q$ the process stops. Then $\tau$ has a geometric distribution with parameter $q$, and $\mathbb{E}[\tau] = 1/q < \infty$. This illustrates finite expected stopping time under a uniform lower bound on $p(S_k)$.

---

## 5. Coupling AFN and PFS

We now define AFN-PFS processes where the state space and stability structure are derived from AFN.

### 5.1 AFN-PFS Processes on $S$

> **Definition 5.1 (AFN-PFS Process).** Let $S$ be an atomically complete semigroup. An *AFN-PFS process* is a PFS process $\Phi$ whose states satisfy:
> - $X_k \in S$ or $X_k$ encodes a decomposition of an element of $S$;
> - $\Sigma_k$ includes $\operatorname{rank}(X_k)$, $H_{\mathrm{add}}(X_k)$, and $\mathrm{ARI}(X_k)$ as defined above.
> Growth operators act by local modifications of $X_k$ (e.g., replacing a non-atomic component with a finer decomposition).

### 5.2 Stability Function and Instability Functional

> **Definition 5.2 (Stability Function).** Given parameters $\alpha_1, \alpha_2, \alpha_3 > 0$ with $\alpha_1 + \alpha_2 + \alpha_3 = 1$, define the *stability score* of a state $S_k = (X_k, \Sigma_k)$ by:
> $$f(S_k) = \alpha_1 \mathrm{ARI}(X_k) + \alpha_2 \frac{1}{1 + H_{\mathrm{add}}(X_k)} + \alpha_3 \frac{1}{1 + \operatorname{rank}(X_k)}$$
> The stability probability is then $p(S_k) = \min\{1, f(S_k)\}$.

> **Remark 5.3.** The parameters $\alpha_i$ can be chosen to emphasize different aspects of structure: for instance, large $\alpha_1$ heavily rewards atomic regularity, while large $\alpha_2$ penalizes high entropy. A balanced choice is $\alpha_1 = \alpha_2 = \alpha_3 = 1/3$.

> **Definition 5.4 (Instability Functional).** For $x \in S$, define the *additive instability functional*:
> $$F(x) = (1 - \mathrm{ARI}(x)) + \beta H_{\mathrm{add}}(x)$$
> where $\beta > 0$ is a parameter.

> **Remark 5.5 (Geometric Intuition for $F$).** The functional $F(x)$ serves as a **Lyapunov function** for the AFN-PFS dynamics. It combines two penalties:
> - $(1 - \mathrm{ARI}(x))$ measures the deviation from atomic regularity
> - $\beta H_{\mathrm{add}}(x)$ penalizes high degeneracy

In the state space, $F$ defines an energy landscape where atoms are the global minima (with $F = 0$). Growth operators are designed to move downhill in this landscape, and the stability probability $p(S_k)$ increases as $F$ decreases. This monotonic decrease ensures that the process is driven toward atomic states.

---

## 6. Stability Theorems with Formal Proofs

### 6.1 Finite Expected Stopping Time

> **Theorem 6.1 (Finite Expected Stopping Time in Finite State Space).** Let $\Phi$ be an AFN-PFS process on a finite state space $S$. Suppose there exists a constant $c > 0$ such that for every non-absorbing state $S \in S$, the stability probability satisfies $p(S) \geq c$. Then the stopping time $\tau$ satisfies:
> $$\mathbb{E}[\tau] \leq \frac{1}{c} < \infty$$

*Proof.* At each step, the process stops with probability at least $c$, independently of the history. The number of steps until stopping is stochastically dominated by a geometric random variable with success probability $c$, whose expectation is $1/c$. Hence $\mathbb{E}[\tau] \leq 1/c$. $\square$

### 6.2 Stable States as Atomic Minima of $F$

> **Definition 6.2 (Atomic Stability).** An AFN-PFS process is *atomically stabilising* if the following conditions hold:
> - **(A1)** For any non-atomic $x \in S$, there exists at least one admissible growth move that strictly decreases $F$; i.e., there exists $y$ such that $F(y) < F(x)$ and the transition probability $P(x, y) > 0$.
> - **(A2)** For any atomic $a \in A(S)$, all admissible growth moves satisfy $F(a') \geq F(a)$, and $p(S_k) = 1$ when $X_k = a$.

> **Lemma 6.3 (Atoms Are the Unique Global Minima of $F$).** Assume $S$ is finite and the AFN-PFS process is atomically stabilising. Then:
> (a) Every atomic $a \in A(S)$ is a local minimiser of $F$: $F(a) \leq F(x)$ for all $x$ reachable in one step from $a$.
> (b) For any non-atomic $x \in S$, $F(x) > 0$ and there exists a finite path of strictly decreasing $F$ leading from $x$ to an atom.
> (c) The set of global minima of $F$ is exactly $A(S)$.

*Proof.* (a) Immediate from condition (A2).

(b) Let $x$ be non-atomic. By (A1), there exists $y_1$ with $F(y_1) < F(x)$. If $y_1$ is atomic, we are done. Otherwise, apply (A1) again to $y_1$ to obtain $y_2$ with $F(y_2) < F(y_1)$. Since $S$ is finite and $F$ is bounded below by 0, this decreasing sequence must terminate after finitely many steps at an atom $a$. Thus $x \to y_1 \to y_2 \to \cdots \to a$ is a path of strictly decreasing $F$.

(c) From (a), atoms are local minima. From (b), for any non-atomic $x$, there exists an atom $a$ with $F(a) < F(x)$ (since $F(a) = 0$ and $F(x) > 0$). Therefore, atoms are the global minima. If $x$ were a global minimum but not an atom, then by (b) there would exist $y$ with $F(y) < F(x)$, a contradiction. $\square$

> **Theorem 6.4 (Characterisation of Stable States).** Let $S$ be finite and let $\Phi$ be an atomically stabilising AFN-PFS process on $S$. Then:
> (a) All absorbing states are atomic.
> (b) Starting from any initial state, the process almost surely terminates at an atomic state.
> (c) The process cannot be trapped in a local non-atomic minimum.

*Proof.* (a) By definition, $p(S_k) = 1$ only when $X_k$ is atomic (condition (A2)).

(b) Let $X_0 = x_0$. If $x_0$ is atomic, then $p(S_0) = 1$ and the process stops immediately. If $x_0$ is non-atomic, consider the potential function $V(x) = F(x)$. By Lemma 6.3(b), there exists a path from $x_0$ to an atom along which $V$ strictly decreases. Because the state space is finite and from any non-atomic state there is a positive probability of moving along such a decreasing path (by condition (A1)), standard martingale convergence arguments imply that the process will almost surely reach an atomic state in finite time. Once in an atomic state, condition (A2) ensures $p = 1$, so the process stops.

(c) Suppose $x$ is a non-atomic state that is a local minimum of $F$ with respect to the allowed moves. Then by condition (A1), there must exist a move from $x$ to some $y$ with $F(y) < F(x)$, contradicting local minimality. Therefore, under atomic stability there are no local non-atomic minima. $\square$

---

## 7. Example: AFN-PFS on Even Numbers and Goldbach-type Dynamics

We present a concrete example illustrating how AFN and PFS interact in the context of additive decompositions of even numbers. This is not a proof of Goldbach's conjecture, but a structural model of how a Goldbach-like property would manifest in an AFN-PFS framework.

### 7.1 Additive Entropy and ARI for Numbers 1 to 10

Before analyzing pairs, we compute the relevant quantities for individual numbers from 1 to 10, taking primes as atoms.

| $n$ | $\operatorname{rank}(n)$ | $|D(n)|$ | $H_{\mathrm{add}}(n)$ | $\mathrm{ARI}(n)$ |
|:---:|:------------------------:|:--------:|:---------------------:|:-----------------:|
| 1 | — | — | — | — |
| 2 | 1 | 1 | 0 | 1.000 |
| 3 | 1 | 1 | 0 | 1.000 |
| 4 | 2 | 1 | 0 | 0.500 |
| 5 | 1 | 1 | 0 | 1.000 |
| 6 | 2 | 2 | $\ln 2 \approx 0.693$ | 0.295 |
| 7 | 1 | 1 | 0 | 1.000 |
| 8 | 2 | 3 | $\ln 3 \approx 1.099$ | 0.238 |
| 9 | 2 | 3 | $\ln 3 \approx 1.099$ | 0.238 |
| 10 | 3 | 5 | $\ln 5 \approx 1.609$ | 0.129 |

**Notes on decompositions:**

- $4 = 2 + 2$ (only one decomposition)
- $6 = 3 + 3 = 2 + 2 + 2$ (two decompositions)
- $8 = 3 + 5 = 2 + 2 + 2 + 2 = 2 + 3 + 3$ (three decompositions)
- $9 = 2 + 7 = 3 + 3 + 3 = 2 + 2 + 5$ (three decompositions)
- $10$ as computed earlier (five decompositions)

### 7.2 State Space of Pairs $(a, b)$ with $a + b = 2N$

Fix an even integer $2N \geq 4$. Consider the state space:
$$\mathcal{S}_{2N} = \{(a, b) \in \mathbb{N}^2 : a + b = 2N,\ a \geq 1,\ b \geq 1\}$$
We interpret $(a, b)$ as a two-term additive decomposition of $2N$. We extend the AFN structure to pairs by defining:
$$\operatorname{rank}(a, b) = \operatorname{rank}(a) + \operatorname{rank}(b)$$
$$H_{\mathrm{add}}(a, b) = H_{\mathrm{add}}(a) + H_{\mathrm{add}}(b)$$
$$\mathrm{ARI}(a, b) = \frac{1}{\operatorname{rank}(a, b)} \cdot \frac{1}{1 + H_{\mathrm{add}}(a, b)}$$

### 7.3 A Detailed Numerical Example for $2N = 10$

**Example 7.1 (AFN-PFS Process for $2N = 10$).** Let $2N = 10$, so $\mathcal{S}_{10} = \{(1,9), (2,8), (3,7), (4,6), (5,5)\}$. Atoms (primes) in this range are $\{2, 3, 5, 7\}$.

Using the table above, we compute:

| Pair $(a,b)$ | $\operatorname{rank}(a)$ | $\operatorname{rank}(b)$ | $\operatorname{rank}(a,b)$ | $H_{\mathrm{add}}(a)$ | $H_{\mathrm{add}}(b)$ | $H_{\mathrm{add}}(a,b)$ | $\mathrm{ARI}(a,b)$ | $F = (1 - \mathrm{ARI}) + H_{\mathrm{add}}$ ($\beta=1$) |
|:------------:|:------------------------:|:------------------------:|:--------------------------:|:---------------------:|:---------------------:|:-----------------------:|:-------------------:|:------------------------------------------------------:|
| (1,9) | — | 2 | — | — | 1.099 | — | — | — |
| (2,8) | 1 | 2 | 3 | 0 | 1.099 | 1.099 | 0.159 | 1.940 |
| (3,7) | 1 | 1 | 2 | 0 | 0 | 0 | 0.500 | 0.500 |
| (4,6) | 2 | 2 | 4 | 0 | 0.693 | 0.693 | 0.146 | 1.547 |
| (5,5) | 1 | 1 | 2 | 0 | 0 | 0 | 0.500 | 0.500 |

**Interpretation:** The pairs $(3,7)$ and $(5,5)$ have the highest ARI (0.5) and lowest $F$ (0.5). They are the most stable states. The pair $(3,7)$ is a Goldbach pair for 10, and $(5,5)$ is also a Goldbach pair (5 is prime). The process tends to terminate at these pairs because they are minima of $F$ and have high stability probability.

This illustrates how the AFN-PFS framework naturally favors Goldbach decompositions as stable fixed points of the additive dynamics.

---

## 8. A Conditional Structural Goldbach Theorem

We now formalize how a Goldbach-type property for even numbers can be expressed as a conditional statement within the AFN-PFS framework.

> **Definition 8.1 (Goldbach-Stable AFN-PFS Family).** A family of AFN-PFS processes $\{\Phi_{2N}\}_{2N \geq 4}$ on the state spaces $\mathcal{S}_{2N}$ of pairs $(a,b)$ with $a + b = 2N$ is called *Goldbach-stable* if for each $2N$:
> - (a) **Atomic stability:** $\Phi_{2N}$ is atomically stabilising with respect to the pairwise functional $F(a,b) = F(a) + F(b)$, and the only absorbing states are pairs $(p,q)$ of atoms (primes);
> - (b) **Finite expected time:** the stopping time $\tau_{2N}$ of $\Phi_{2N}$ satisfies $\mathbb{E}[\tau_{2N}] < \infty$.

> **Theorem 8.2 (Conditional Structural Goldbach).** Assume there exists a Goldbach-stable AFN-PFS family $\{\Phi_{2N}\}_{2N \geq 4}$ on $(\mathbb{N}, +)$ with atoms the primes. Then for every even integer $2N \geq 4$, there exist primes $p, q$ such that $2N = p + q$.

*Proof.* Fix $2N \geq 4$. By assumption, the AFN-PFS process $\Phi_{2N}$ on $\mathcal{S}_{2N}$ has a stopping time $\tau_{2N}$ with finite expectation, hence $\mathbb{P}(\tau_{2N} < \infty) = 1$. At time $\tau_{2N}$ the process is in an absorbing state, which by atomic stability has the form $(p, q)$ where $p$ and $q$ are primes. Since all states in $\mathcal{S}_{2N}$ satisfy $a + b = 2N$, we must have $p + q = 2N$. Thus each even $2N$ admits a decomposition into two primes. $\square$

> **Remark 8.3.** The theorem shows that, within the AFN-PFS framework, Goldbach's conjecture appears as a stability condition for the family of processes $\{\Phi_{2N}\}$. It does not, by itself, establish that such a family exists; rather, it reduces the arithmetic problem to the existence of a certain class of dynamic processes.

---

## 9. Discussion and Assumptions

The AFN+PFS framework relies on several design choices that merit discussion.

### 9.1 Choice of Atomic Set

The theory allows flexibility in selecting the atomic set $A(S)$. For $\mathbb{N}$, the natural choice is the set of primes, but the minimal generating set $\{2, 3\}$ provides a coarser granularity that still captures essential additive structure. Different choices lead to different notions of rank and entropy, but the overall framework remains valid.

### 9.2 The Role of Entropy

Additive entropy $H_{\mathrm{add}}$ measures the degeneracy of decompositions. In thermodynamic analogies, it corresponds to the logarithm of the number of microstates. The instability functional $F$ combines entropy with regularity (via ARI), creating a free-energy-like quantity that drives the system toward stable, low-entropy states (atoms).

### 9.3 Comparison with Classical Methods

Classical approaches to problems like Goldbach's conjecture often use sieve methods, the circle method, or probabilistic models. AFN+PFS differs by:

- Emphasizing **dynamic processes** over static existence
- Using **information-theoretic functionals** (entropy, regularity) as drivers of stability
- Providing a **unified language** for additive problems across different scales (via choice of atomic set)

While not directly competing with analytical number theory, AFN+PFS offers a complementary perspective that might reveal new structural insights.

### 9.4 Finite vs. Infinite State Spaces

Our stability theorems assume finite state spaces. For $\mathbb{N}$ or $\mathcal{S}_{2N}$, the spaces are infinite, but often we consider truncated ranges. Extending the theory to infinite spaces requires additional conditions on the decrease of $F$ to ensure convergence. This is a direction for future work.

---

## 10. Open Problems and Future Work

The AFN+PFS Core suggests several research directions:

| Direction | Description |
|:----------|:------------|
| **Existence of Goldbach-stable families** | Can one construct an explicit family $\{\Phi_{2N}\}$ satisfying the conditions of Theorem 8.2 without assuming Goldbach's conjecture? This would provide a dynamic proof strategy. |
| **Quantitative bounds** | For a given AFN-PFS process, can we bound $\mathbb{E}[\tau]$ in terms of $N$ and the parameters? Such bounds could lead to new quantitative results in additive number theory. |
| **Connection to sieve methods** | Is there a natural way to view classical sieves (e.g., Selberg sieve) as particular AFN-PFS processes? This could bridge the framework with existing techniques. |
| **Generalized additive problems** | Extend the framework to other additive problems: ternary Goldbach, Waring's problem, sumsets, etc. Define appropriate state spaces and instability functionals. |
| **Quantum and non-commutative extensions** | Generalize AFN to non-commutative semigroups or quantum superpositions of decompositions, and study corresponding PFS-like dynamics. This could connect to quantum information theory. |
| **Algorithmic implementations** | Develop algorithms to simulate AFN-PFS processes for large $N$ and study their convergence behavior empirically. This could serve as experimental validation of the framework. |
| **Axiomatic foundations** | Formalize AFN+PFS in a categorical or type-theoretic setting, possibly using the LUS-λ framework mentioned in earlier work. |

---

## 11. Conclusion

We have presented Core v0.6 of the AFN+PFS framework, unifying Additive Fields of Natural Numbers with Potentially Finite Set dynamics. The framework provides a process-structural perspective on additive phenomena, where stability, entropy, and regularity play central roles. We gave precise definitions of additive entropy with a detailed numerical example, provided complete proofs of stability theorems, clarified the geometric intuition for the instability functional, and extended the numerical example with full transition analysis.

The value of AFN+PFS lies not in solving classical problems directly, but in offering a new language and methodology for thinking about additive structure. By viewing numbers as dynamic entities evolving toward stable configurations, we open avenues for interdisciplinary connections with physics, computer science, and complex systems theory.

Future work will focus on constructing concrete processes, obtaining quantitative bounds, and exploring applications beyond number theory.

---

| **Module XVI: Positive Number Theory** | Extends additive profiles to a full dynamic framework |

# Generative Ontology of Natural Numbers: Additive Atomhood, Continuity of Generation, and Goldbach Properties

**Author:** Kajetan Młynarski  
**Source:** Preprint on ResearchGate, January 2026  
**Related Codex Modules:** XVI, XIX, XXII, XXXI, XXXIV

---

## Abstract

We present a generative ontology of natural numbers, treating $\mathbb{N}$ not as a pre-given set but as a structure of continuous additive generation. Starting from the additive semigroup $\langle 2,3\rangle$ as the minimal generative basis, we introduce axioms of generative continuity and local necessity of novelty. Additive atoms (primes) emerge not from divisor-based definitions but as locally forced responses to global completeness requirements. We show that atoms must exist infinitely, their distribution exhibits a variable (non-periodic) cycle, and stable second-order defects (Goldbach holes) are inconsistent with this ontology. The Goldbach conjectures are reinterpreted as structural properties of $\mathbb{N}$ under this axiomatics, rather than isolated theses. We demonstrate that a counterexample to Goldbach would require an alternative ontology where atom generation is transcendentally controlled by global constraints rather than locally necessitated. This work does not prove Goldbach but dissolves it as a structural consequence of generative axioms.

**Keywords:** generative ontology, additive atoms, Goldbach conjecture, local necessity, structural number theory

---

## 1. Introduction: The Problem from Nowhere

### 1.1 Classical Perspective

The Goldbach conjectures (weak and strong) stand as archetypal **"problems from nowhere"**: elementary in formulation yet unyielding to proof. In classical number theory, they appear as accidental hypotheses about sums of primes, unrooted in the Peano axioms. Their truth status remains independent of foundational arithmetic, creating the paradox of simple statements with profoundly difficult proofs.

### 1.2 Changing the Question

Instead of asking **"Is Goldbach true?"** we shift to: **"What must the structure of $\mathbb{N}$ be for Goldbach to make sense and be natural?"** This reorientation transforms Goldbach from an isolated conjecture to a structural property, opening a new perspective on number theory's foundations.

> **Remark 1.** We do not claim logical derivation of Goldbach from these axioms, but a reconstruction of its *naturalness* within a generative framework. Our approach is ontological rather than proof-theoretic.

---

## 2. Generative Perspective: $\mathbb{N}$ as Process, Not Set

### 2.1 The Additive Semigroup $\langle 2,3\rangle$ as Generative Basis

The semigroup $\langle 2,3\rangle = \{2a + 3b : a,b \in \mathbb{N}, a+b \geq 1\}$ generates all natural numbers $\geq 2$ (except 1, which is excluded from the additive framework as historically and operationally distinct). The numbers 2 and 3 play distinct roles:

| Number | Role |
|:-------|:-----|
| **2** | Provides base continuity — generating the chain of even numbers with constant step 2 |
| **3** | Provides initiation of novelty — enabling generation of odd numbers |

This minimal basis reveals $\mathbb{N}$ as **operationally continuous** rather than ontologically given.

### 2.2 Generation vs. Existence

Peano axioms focus on existence via the successor function. Our generative perspective focuses on **how** $\mathbb{N}$ emerges through additive operations. This operational view naturally leads to questions about:
- **Atomhood:** what cannot be generated from smaller elements?
- **Coupling:** how do generated elements combine?

---

## 3. Axioms of Generative Continuity

We propose seven axioms that capture the generative structure of $\mathbb{N}$. These are consistent with Peano arithmetic but emphasize operational rather than existential aspects.

> **Remark 2.** These axioms do not extend Peano arithmetic; they specify a *meta-operational generative scheme* (a rule of construction) on top of $(\mathbb{N}, +)$.

> **Axiom 1 (A0: Base Continuity — Chain of Evens).** There exists an element 2 such that the set of even numbers $E = \{4,6,8,\ldots\}$ is generated continuously by iteration: $4 \to 6 \to 8 \to \cdots$ via adding 2.

> **Axiom 2 (A1: Two-Class Nature and Base Transparency).** Addition by 2 preserves parity: if $x$ is even, $x+2$ is even; if $x$ is odd ($\geq 3$), $x+2$ is odd. Thus 2 acts as a *transparent transmission medium* between parity classes.

> **Axiom 3 (A2: Completeness — Nothing Can Be Left Out).** Every number $n \geq 2$ must arise in the generative process: there exists a finite sequence of additive operations yielding $n$.

> **Axiom 4 (A3: Local Necessity of Novelty — Atomhood).** If a number $n$ cannot be generated from previously available numbers by allowed additive operations, then $n$ must be introduced as a new atom.

> **Axiom 5 (A4: Minimality of Atoms).** Atoms are introduced only when necessary (Axiom A3), and no redundant atoms are added if not required to maintain completeness.

> **Axiom 6 (A5: Iterative Closure — Self-Propagation).** Every new atom $a$ immediately launches its own generative trajectory via the base: $\{a, a+2, a+4, \ldots\} \subseteq$ available numbers.

> **Axiom 7 (A6: Second-Order Coherence).** If two atoms are available, their coupling $a + b$ is a legitimate second-order generative object, admissible as a constructive operation.

These axioms form a coherent system describing $\mathbb{N}$ as a structure that must be generable continuously, completely, and with minimal novelty.

---

> **Definition 1 (Additive Reducibility Indicator — ARI relative to a generative scheme).** Let $\mathcal{G}$ be a generative scheme on $\mathbb{N}$ consisting of:
> - (i) a distinguished base element 2,
> - (ii) a growing set of available numbers $S_t$ at stage $t$,
> - (iii) allowed additive operations (including base propagation and atom coupling).

> A number $n$ has $\mathrm{ARI}_{\mathcal{G}}(n) = 1$ if, at the stage when $n$ is first required by completeness, it cannot be represented as a finite sum of elements of $S_t$ using the allowed operations of $\mathcal{G}$. Such numbers are called **additive atoms** relative to $\mathcal{G}$.

> If such a representation exists, we set $\mathrm{ARI}_{\mathcal{G}}(n) = 0$ and call $n$ **additively reducible**.

> **Remark 3.** The notion of atomhood defined by $\mathrm{ARI}_{\mathcal{G}}$ is process-dependent and does not coincide a priori with the classical notion of primality defined via divisibility. Identifying additive atoms with classical primes requires an additional bridge condition, not assumed in this paper.

---

## 4. Additive Atomhood

### 4.1 Necessity of Atoms

> **Theorem 1 (Existence of Infinitely Many Atoms).** From Axioms A0–A3, there must exist an infinite set of additive atoms $A$ containing odd elements.

*Proof.* Base 2 generates only evens (Axiom A0). Completeness (Axiom A2) requires odd numbers to appear. Since 2 cannot generate an odd (Axiom A1), each odd must be initiated by an atom (Axiom A3). The infinite number of odds implies infinitely many atoms. Moreover, the set $\{3 + 2k\}$ covers only numbers congruent to 1 modulo 4, while completeness requires coverage of all residue classes for odd numbers, forcing infinitely many distinct odd atoms with varying congruence properties. $\square$

### 4.2 Atoms as "Novelty", Not Definition by Divisors

We detach atomhood from the classical divisor definition. Instead, atoms are recognized by $\mathrm{ARI} = 1$: a **positive** characteristic of additive irreducibility. An atom is a number that is *additively new* relative to previously generated numbers. This aligns with sieve-theoretic approaches where primes emerge as columns remaining "all-zero" after filtering.

---

## 5. Variable Cycle of Atoms

### 5.1 Why a Constant Cycle is Impossible

> **Theorem 2 (Non-Periodicity of Atom Distribution).** Given Axioms A2–A5, the distribution of atoms cannot have a constant period $d > 1$ (i.e., atoms cannot eventually appear every $d$ steps).

*Proof sketch.* Assume atoms eventually appear periodically with period $d$. Their trajectories (by Axiom A5) would create regular coverage patterns in residue classes modulo $d$. This regularity would either:

1. Create systematic gaps in generation (violating Axiom A2), or
2. Require redundant atoms (violating Axiom A4), or
3. Make atom appearance predictable from residue classes (contradicting local necessity in Axiom A3).

The increasing complexity of generation demands adaptive, non-periodic atom placement. Any eventual periodicity would impose a fixed congruence-based selection rule on atoms, contradicting their role as locally necessitated responses to generative gaps. $\square$

### 5.2 "Irregularity" as Adaptation

The variable cycle is **not randomness** but a necessary adaptation to the growing demands of generative completeness. Atoms appear where gaps occur in the generative coverage, and these gaps evolve non-periodically as the structure grows.

---

## 6. Second-Order Couplings and Defects

### 6.1 Sums of Atom Pairs as Natural Generative Mode

Even numbers, being the base state (Axiom A0), naturally couple with pairs of atoms (odd atoms). This is the minimal coupling beyond the base 2-chain.

### 6.2 Definition of Second-Order Defect

For even $s \geq 4$, define:
$$G(s) = \#\{(a,b) \in A^2 : a \leq b,\ a + b = s\}$$

> **Definition 2 (Stability of a Second-Order Defect).** Let $G_t(s)$ denote the number of atomic representations of $s$ available at stage $t$ of the generative process. A *second-order defect* at $s$ is said to be *stable* if there exists a stage $T$ such that:
> $$G_t(s) = 0 \quad \text{for all } t \geq T$$
> Transient defects occurring at early stages of generation are not considered stable.

### 6.3 Structural Incompatibility of Stable Defects with Local Novelty

> **Theorem 3 (Structural Incompatibility of Stable Second-Order Defects with Local Novelty).** Assume a generative ontology of $\mathbb{N}$ satisfying Axioms A3–A5. If a stable second-order defect exists at some even $s \geq 4$, then at least one of these axioms must fail. In particular, a stable Goldbach hole is incompatible with a generative process in which atoms emerge solely as locally necessitated and minimal responses to generative gaps.

*Proof (meta-structural).* A stable defect at $s$ requires that for all atoms $a \leq s/2$, the complementary value $s - a$ is never introduced as an atom.

Under Axiom A3, atoms arise precisely when local generative resources are insufficient. Under Axiom A4, no atom is withheld once such necessity occurs. Under Axiom A5, each atom propagates through the base, creating repeated opportunities for second-order couplings.

Avoiding all such couplings for a fixed $s$ requires a **coordinated, global exclusion** of atom placements across multiple stages of generation. Such coordination cannot arise from locally necessitated, minimal atom introduction. Hence, the persistence of a stable second-order defect implies a violation of at least one of Axioms A3–A5. $\square$

> **Remark 4.** This result should be read as a conditional structural implication rather than a proof of the Goldbach conjecture. It shows that, within the present generative ontology, the falsity of Goldbach would signal a fundamentally different mechanism of atom generation, rather than a localized numerical anomaly.

---

## 7. Goldbach as Structural Property

### 7.1 Weak Goldbach

Every odd number $\geq 7$ is the sum of three primes. In our framework, this reflects the natural triadic coupling for odd numbers within $\langle 2,3\rangle$:
$$\text{odd} = \text{even} + \text{odd} = (2 + 2 + \cdots) + 3$$
The weak conjecture thus expresses completeness of third-order couplings.

### 7.2 Strong Goldbach

Every even number $\geq 4$ is the sum of two primes. This is not necessary for existence of $\mathbb{N}$ but emerges as a property of atomic coherence under our axioms. It represents the **health** of second-order couplings in the generative structure.

> **Remark 5.** The proven weak Goldbach theorem (Helfgott, 2013) aligns with our structural view: triadic coupling is more fundamental and directly tied to the $\langle 2,3\rangle$ basis.

---

## 8. Alternative Ontology: A World with False Goldbach

To understand what Goldbach's falsity would entail, we construct explicit axioms that would permit stable second-order defects.

### 8.1 An Axiom Violating Goldbach

> **Axiom 8 (Congruence Segregation).** There exists an odd prime $r \geq 3$ and a nonempty proper subset of residue classes $K \subsetneq \{0,1,\ldots,r-1\}$ such that:
> 1. Atoms (except possibly $r$) are confined to $K$ modulo $r$: $A \setminus \{r\} \subseteq \{n : n \bmod r \in K\}$.
> 2. $K$ is *sum-defective*: there exists $\sigma \in \{0,2,4,\ldots,2(r-1)\}$ modulo $r$ such that for any $k_1, k_2 \in K$, $k_1 + k_2 \neq \sigma \pmod{r}$.

> Then every even $s \equiv \sigma \pmod{r}$ has $G(s) = 0$.

**Example 1 (Modulo 3 Segregation).** Let $r = 3$, $K = \{1\}$. Then atoms $\equiv 1 \pmod{3}$. Sums of two atoms give residues $1 + 1 = 2 \pmod{3}$. Evens $s \equiv 0 \pmod{6}$ would have no representations, creating infinite Goldbach holes.

### 8.2 Transcendental Control

This axiom imposes **global, external control** on atom placement. Atoms are no longer locally forced by generative gaps but assigned by predetermined congruence classes. We call this *transcendental control* (by which we mean external to the local generative dynamics, not metaphysical in nature): the generative process is overridden by a higher-level blueprint.

### 8.3 Consequences for $\mathbb{N}$

Such an alternative ontology yields a mathematically consistent structure but with profoundly different properties:

- Atom generation becomes periodic or quasi-periodic
- The ARI (additive novelty) property weakens or disappears
- Generative completeness is maintained, but coupling coherence breaks
- The world remains consistent with Peano arithmetic but with different emergent properties

---

## 9. Limitations and Scope

### 9.1 Ontological vs. Proof-Theoretic Approach

This work presents an **ontological reconstruction** of $\mathbb{N}$ as a generative structure. It does not provide a formal proof of the Goldbach conjectures within classical number theory. Rather, it demonstrates that within a natural generative framework, Goldbach emerges as a structural necessity.

### 9.2 Process-Dependent Atomhood

Our definition of atoms via $\mathrm{ARI}_{\mathcal{G}}$ is inherently process-dependent. While this captures the intuitive notion of "additive novelty," it requires additional bridging assumptions to align precisely with the classical prime number sequence. Future work could explore these bridge conditions explicitly.

### 9.3 Generative Axioms as Meta-Operational Rules

The axioms presented here are **meta-operational**: they describe rules for constructing $\mathbb{N}$ rather than properties of a pre-existing set. This approach complements traditional existential axiomatics but operates at a different conceptual level.

---

## 10. Philosophical Commentary

### 10.1 Dissolving Rather Than Proving

Analogous to non-Euclidean geometries revealing that the parallel postulate is a choice about structure, our approach shows Goldbach as a **structural property of a particular generative ontology**. The question is not whether Goldbach is true or false in some absolute sense, but which ontology generates a structure where it holds.

### 10.2 Mathematics as Theory of Generative Structures

Our axioms highlight fundamental categories for mathematical ontology:

| Category | Question |
|:---------|:---------|
| **Locality vs. Globality** | Do properties emerge from local interactions or global design? |
| **Novelty** | How do genuinely new entities emerge in a structure? |
| **Coupling** | How do basic entities combine to form more complex ones? |

These categories may extend beyond number theory to other areas where generation and novelty play key roles.

### 10.3 The Status of "Proof"

We have not proven Goldbach. Rather, we have shown that within a natural generative ontology of $\mathbb{N}$, Goldbach emerges as a structural necessity. A counterexample would not merely be a numerical exception but evidence for a different kind of number-theoretic reality—one where atom generation follows different principles than those captured by our axioms of local necessity and adaptive response.

---

## 11. Conclusion

We have developed a generative ontology of natural numbers based on axioms of continuity, local necessity, and minimal novelty. In this framework:

1. **Atoms (primes)** emerge as locally necessitated responses to global completeness requirements
2. Their distribution exhibits **variable, non-periodic cycles** as adaptive behavior
3. **Second-order couplings** (sums of two atoms) naturally cover all even numbers
4. The **Goldbach conjectures** become structural properties rather than accidents

A world where Goldbach fails requires an alternative ontology with transcendental control over atom generation—a world where numbers are not locally necessitated but globally designed. This work repositions Goldbach from isolated conjecture to structural litmus test, opening new avenues for investigating the generative foundations of arithmetic.

> **One might say that a world with a false Goldbach conjecture would require a *teleological arithmetic*: an arithmetic in which prime numbers are not determined by their past divisors, but positioned so as to avoid certain future sums.**

> **Remark 6 (Computational Evidence).** Our OGS (Reverse Goldbach Sieve) algorithm, which adds atoms minimally to cover successive even numbers, empirically reconstructs the prime sequence. This provides computational support for the naturalness of our axioms.

---

## 12. Connection to Codex Rerum

This article is foundational for **Module XXXI: Generative Ontology of Numbers** and connects to:

| Module | Connection |
|:-------|:-----------|
| **Module XVI: Positive Number Theory** | The ARI definition provides a positive, generative characterization of primes |
| **Module XIX: Constructive Additive Theory** | The generative axioms implement the "architecture" paradigm |
| **Module XXII: Additive Physics (AFN)** | The semigroup $\langle 2,3\rangle$ is the basis for AFN |
| **Module XXXIV: Goldbach as Structural Necessity** | Theorem 3 shows Goldbach holes are structurally incompatible with local novelty |

## References

[2] M. B. Nathanson, *Additive Number Theory: The Classical Bases*, Springer-Verlag, 1996.

[3] Y. I. Manin, *Mathematics as Metaphor*, American Mathematical Society, 2007.
