# Observations on Coloring n-Dimensional Maps

**Author:** Kajetan Młynarski  
**Source:** Preprint on ResearchGate  
**Related Codex Modules:** IX, XVII, XXVII

---

## Abstract

This article presents a novel approach to the problem of coloring maps in various dimensions, encompassing both classical two-dimensional maps with two-dimensional territories and more abstract cases, such as one-dimensional and zero-dimensional territories. The discussion begins with simple theorems on coloring points on a line and one-dimensional planar maps, leading to an analysis of problems in two-dimensional and higher-dimensional spaces. New tools, such as **collinearization**, are introduced, simplifying the analysis of n-dimensional maps by transforming them into structured forms that allow effective coloring. The exposition includes intuitive examples as well as general mathematical theorems. The key conclusion is a proof that any n-dimensional map of n-dimensional territories homeomorphic to a collinearized map can be colored with at most $2^n$ colors.

**Keywords:** Map coloring, four-color theorem, one-dimensional maps, n-dimensional maps, collinearization, adjacency problems, dimension theory

---

## 1. Introduction

Map coloring is a classical mathematical problem with wide applications in geography, computer graphics, and graph theory. One of the most renowned results in this field is the **four-color theorem**, which states that any planar two-dimensional map can be colored with at most four colors, such that no two adjacent territories share the same color. This problem, though extensively studied, serves as inspiration for exploring more general cases, including maps in higher dimensions and territories with unconventional geometric structures.

In this article, a novel approach to map coloring is proposed, avoiding traditional references to graph theory and focusing on the **geometric structure of space and adjacency relationships**. The discussion begins with analyses of zero-dimensional and one-dimensional planar maps, followed by considerations of two-dimensional maps, utilizing classical results in a new context. In the later sections, the concept of **collinearization** is introduced as a tool for studying n-dimensional maps, enabling simplified analysis and proving that any n-dimensional map with n-dimensional territories, homeomorphic to a collinearized map, can be colored with $2^n$ colors.

> **Key Insight:** The maximum number of colors needed for a map is determined not by the complexity of adjacency relations alone, but by the number of "boundaries" or "contact points" that territories can have. This geometric perspective unifies coloring problems across dimensions.

---

## 2. One-Dimensional Maps

If we divide a non-intersecting line into segments that touch only at their endpoints, we can treat it as a **one-dimensional planar map**. Let us observe that the maximum number of colors required to distinguish territories ("countries") does not depend on the specific adjacency relations but rather on how many countries can simultaneously be adjacent to one another, i.e., each to each.

> **Theorem 1 (Coloring Segments on a Line).** Segments touching at their endpoints, into which a non-intersecting line has been divided, can be colored using only **two colors**, such that no two segments of the same color are adjacent.

*Proof.* Each segment has only two endpoints (boundaries) and divides the line into isolated parts. Thus, a simple algorithm ensures coloring with just two colors. We select any segment and assign it color A. The segments adjacent to it are assigned color B. In the next step, we assign color A to segments adjacent to those already colored with B. Repeating this procedure, we can color all the segments into which the line has been divided.

Since each segment has only two possible boundaries, it can only touch two other segments, and these are isolated. Therefore, the theorem holds. $\square$

> **Theorem 2 (Coloring Pairs of Segments).** All pairs of segments into which a line has been divided can be colored using at most **four colors**, such that no pair of segments of the same color is adjacent to another pair of the same color.

*Proof.* Each pair has at most four endpoints that can touch the segments of other pairs, and hence, at most four pairs can be mutually adjacent (each to each).

First, color all the segments alternately with two colors, for example, A and B, as in the previous proof. This ensures that no two adjacent segments will share the same color.

Observe: Each pair consists of two segments, each of which is colored either A or B. There are four possible color combinations in a pair:

| Combination | Meaning |
|:------------|:--------|
| AA | Both segments are of color A |
| AB | First segment A, second segment B |
| BA | First segment B, second segment A |
| BB | Both segments are of color B |

Assign a unique color to each combination:

| Combination | Assigned Color |
|:------------|:---------------|
| AA | 1 |
| AB | 2 |
| BA | 3 |
| BB | 4 |

Since adjacent segments have different colors (A and B), when two segments from different pairs touch, their pair color combinations must differ, and thus the pairs receive different colors. Consequently, no two segments from different pairs of the same color will be adjacent. $\square$

> **Theorem 3 (Coloring n-Tuples of Segments).** All $n$-tuples of segments into which a line has been divided can be colored using at most **$2^n$ colors**, such that no $n$-tuple of a given color is adjacent to another $n$-tuple of the same color.

*Proof.* First, observe that each $n$-tuple has at most $2n$ endpoints (boundaries). However, the number of distinct patterns of segment colors within an $n$-tuple is $2^n$ (each of the $n$ segments can be either A or B). Therefore, we can assign a unique color to each pattern. Since adjacent $n$-tuples must differ in the color pattern of their constituent segments (due to the alternating coloring of segments), they receive different colors. Thus, $2^n$ colors suffice. $\square$

---

## 3. Coloring Points on a Line

> **Theorem 4 (Alternating Coloring of Points).** All points on a line can be colored using only **two colors**, such that between any two points of the same color, there is a point of a different color.

*Proof.* We construct a modified version of the Koch curve [8]. The construction starts by dividing a segment into five parts (rather than three, as in the original Koch curve construction). On two of these segments, we construct two upward-open triangles (analogous to the Koch curve construction). The triangles are built on the second and fourth segments of the divided segment, meaning they are separated by the third segment. All points of inflection in the resulting curve are colored alternately with two colors.

In the next step, we repeat this procedure on each segment of the newly formed curve, ensuring that between any two points of the same color, there is a point of a different color. This process is feasible in every subsequent step, which is precisely why we start the construction with two triangles. (Since the number of new inflection points doubles at each iteration, they can always be assigned alternating colors, and the coloring structure remains consistent across iterations.)

Iterating infinitely, we obtain a curve analogous to the Koch curve, where all points are colored in an alternating pattern.

Now, we apply the reverse procedure. On the obtained curve, we identify the inflection points created in the first step of the construction and straighten the triangles, but without removing one of their sides. This causes the base of the curve to extend by two-fifths. We then iterate this procedure infinitely, ultimately obtaining a straight line. Each triangle is gradually flattened by progressively extending its sides until, in the limit, all points lie on a single line. Since the deformation process is continuous and the coloring was assigned at each stage of the construction, in the limit, every pair of points of the same color has a point of a different color between them. $\square$

> **Theorem 5 (Coloring n-Tuples of Points).** All $n$-tuples of points on a line can be colored with at most **$2^n$ colors** such that between any two points of a given $n$-tuple, there are points of colors different from the color of that $n$-tuple.

*Proof.* Each point on a line has exactly two "boundaries," as it divides the line into two parts (one to the left and one to the right). From Theorem 4, we know that points on a line can be colored with two colors (e.g., red C and blue N) such that between any two points of the same color, there is a point of a different color. For an $n$-tuple, the pattern of colors of its constituent points determines a unique color assignment from the $2^n$ possibilities. This ensures separation. $\square$

---

## 4. Two-Dimensional Maps

> **Theorem 6 (Coloring Points in the Plane).** All points in a plane can be colored with **four colors** such that between any point and another point of the same color, there is a point of a different color.

*Proof.* Each point in a quadrant of the Cartesian coordinate system corresponds uniquely to a pair of $(x, y)$ coordinates. As we know, all points on a line can be colored with two colors (e.g., red C and blue N) such that between points of the same color, there is a point of a different color.

Color the axes of the quadrant (constituting a single line) accordingly. Now, draw a line parallel to the y-axis through each point on the x-axis and assign to these lines the colors corresponding to the distinguished points on the x-axis. Proceed analogously for the y-axis.

In this way, at each point in the quadrant, two "color-coordinate lines" intersect, and each point in the plane is uniquely assigned a pair of colors: (C,C), (C,N), (N,C), (N,N). Thus, it suffices to assign distinct colors to the four "color-coordinate pairs" to satisfy the theorem.

This establishes a kind of analogue to the four-color theorem for a two-dimensional map where the "countries" are points, i.e., zero-dimensional entities.

> **Remark.** There are certain analogies between Theorem 6 and problems related to the Hadwiger-Nelson problem [1, 6, 7].

> **Theorem 7 (Coloring One-Dimensional Territories in the Plane).** To color a planar two-dimensional map composed of $n$ one-dimensional territories, at most **$n$ colors** are required.

*Proof.* The proof is very straightforward. Any map formed by a bundle of $n$ segments touching at their endpoints and thus sharing a common boundary requires $n$ colors. Interestingly, while two-dimensional maps with zero-dimensional territories (and, as we know, four-dimensional ones) satisfy the four-color theorem, a map with one-dimensional territories does not. $\square$

### 4.1 The Four-Color Theorem Revisited

The problem of determining the maximum number of colors needed to color two-dimensional maps with two-dimensional territories, known as the **four-color theorem**, is very well known, and its proof has been widely discussed and confirmed [2]. Let us, however, examine this problem from the perspective proposed here, avoiding references to graph theory and utilizing the results established so far.

If we place a finite map in a quadrant of the Cartesian coordinate system, each territory is uniquely represented by two coordinate segments on the axes. Each region of a two-dimensional map is thus represented by two regions on a one-dimensional map. However, this one-dimensional map is not planar in most cases because the coordinate segments overlap.

Furthermore, this procedure is not feasible for all two-dimensional maps with one-dimensional territories. For example, if a "country" is straight and perpendicular to the x- or y-axis, its projection is a point, not a segment. We would thus have a one-dimensional map comprising both one-dimensional and zero-dimensional territories.

To determine the number of colors needed, we would need to introduce a concept of boundaries between points and segments, but such a boundary could neither be of dimension zero nor one.

Let us attempt to **"planarize"** a one-dimensional map representing the colors of a two-dimensional map with two-dimensional territories. The problem involves uniquely arranging coordinate segments linearly so that they neither overlap nor touch except at their endpoints. Assume that on the y-axis, we have overlapping coordinate intervals of two territories. However, if the smallest coordinate on the x-axis for one of these countries is smaller than that of the other, we can arrange them on a modified "color-coordinate" y-axis in the order determined by these sorted values. The same method can be applied to the x-axis.

Using this method, we can "planarize" a one-dimensional map representing a two-dimensional map. It turns out that a two-dimensional map of two-dimensional countries can have a **unique representation as a one-dimensional map**, where each country is represented by two territories. Note that if more than four territories could simultaneously border each other on a two-dimensional map, such a map could not have a unique representation as a one-dimensional map with pairs of segments representing countries. This situation is analogous to the case discussed for two-dimensional maps with one-dimensional territories. Thus, the four-color theorem holds.

---

## 5. n-Dimensional Maps

When addressing the problem of n-dimensional maps, let us first note that even for three-dimensional maps, the number of colors required to color a map of n three-dimensional territories can equal n. This relationship is well-known. A simple "intuitive" proof involves imagining n spheres, each with n long, flexible appendages. We can connect the appendages of each sphere to those of all others. Since all spheres are adjacent to each other, n colors are needed. This also applies to dimensions higher than three.

The problem of coloring multidimensional maps, however, is rich with various intriguing special cases [4, 5]. In the framework presented here, the necessity for n colors arises because even the half-axes of one octant of a three-dimensional Cartesian coordinate system do not form a line but rather a bundle of rays originating from a common point. Consequently, the corresponding coordinate segments are not collinear.

---

## 6. Collinearization

Let us introduce an operation we shall call **collinearization**. If we have a finite three-dimensional map, we can shift the z-axis to the endpoint of its x-coordinate segment. In this way, we obtain a space in which there is a one-to-one correspondence between its points and triples of collinear coordinate points.

> **Definition 6.1 (Collinearization).** Collinearization is a transformation of space and the maps contained within it that aligns coordinate axes linearly, such that each point's coordinates become collinear. This transformation does **not** preserve homeomorphism. For example, all knots, links, and tori become stretched and cut.

**Figure 1:** Collinearization of a three-dimensional space with a torus.

Collinearization can also be performed for n-dimensional spaces by appropriately shifting the coordinate segments. Somewhat similar concepts have already been studied [3].

> **Theorem 8 (Coloring Collinearized Maps).** Any n-dimensional map of n-dimensional territories **homeomorphic to a collinearized map** can be colored with at most **$2^n$ colors**, such that no two territories of the same color are adjacent.

*Proof.* The theorem follows by reasoning analogous to that used to justify the four-color theorem. In a collinearized map, each territory corresponds to a unique pattern of coordinate segments, analogous to the $2^n$ patterns in the one-dimensional case. Since adjacent territories must differ in their coordinate patterns, $2^n$ colors suffice. $\square$

> **Remark.** A collinearized map is, in a sense, half of the original map and is not homeomorphic to it. Collinearization divides (cuts) the stretched original map into two parts. Each of these parts can be colored using at most $2^n$ colors. However, both parts together remain homeomorphic to the original map (since stretching does not affect homeomorphism).

Thus, we can color one part with $2^n$ colors and the other also with $2^n$ colors. If we assign different colors to different halves, then the entire map (which is homeomorphic to the original) can be colored using at most $2 \cdot 2^n = 2^{n+1}$ colors. This means that each region is assigned two colors, with the boundary between these colors running internally within the region along the cutting plane (or space).

> **Theorem 9 (Two-Color Per Region).** All regions of any n-dimensional planar map can be colored in such a way that:
> 1. Each region is assigned **two colors**.
> 2. No region with a given color (one of the two assigned) touches another region with the same color (for either of the two colors).

*Proof.* This follows from the double application of Theorem 8 to the two halves of the collinearized map. $\square$

---

## 7. Summary and Conclusions

This article has presented a unified, geometric approach to map coloring across dimensions:

| Dimension of Territories | Map Type | Maximum Colors | Theorem |
|:------------------------:|:---------|:--------------:|:--------|
| 0 (points) | Line | 2 | Theorem 4 |
| 0 (points) | Plane | 4 | Theorem 6 |
| 1 (segments) | Line | $2^n$ (for n-tuples) | Theorem 3 |
| 1 (segments) | Plane | n | Theorem 7 |
| 2 (regions) | Plane | 4 | Four-Color Theorem |
| n (regions) | n-Dimensional | $2^n$ (collinearized) | Theorem 8 |

**Key contributions:**

1. A purely geometric approach to coloring problems, avoiding graph-theoretic reductions
2. Introduction of **collinearization** as a tool for simplifying n-dimensional maps
3. Proof that collinearized n-dimensional maps require at most $2^n$ colors
4. Demonstration that the four-color theorem emerges as a special case of this geometric perspective

> **Collinearization is a transformation that, while not preserving homeomorphism, preserves the essential adjacency structure needed for coloring. It allows us to reduce higher-dimensional coloring problems to the one-dimensional case, where the $2^n$ bound follows naturally from the pattern of coordinate segments.**
